class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-ion%20batteries">Na-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Carboxylate&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20carboxylate">sodium carboxylate</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Halogenation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_halogenation">halogenation</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anodes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anodes">anodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Organic+Electrode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_organic%20electrode%20materials">organic electrode materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Due to the high demands for portable electronics, electric vehicles, and grid-scale electrical energy storage, it is critical to develop energy storage devices based on low-cost, abundant, and environmentally benign electrode materials to achieve high sustainability and carbon neutrality. However, state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries (LIBs), consisting of graphite anodes and cobalt-rich cathodes, cannot satisfy these demands due to the high cost and limited availability of lithium resources, as well as high cost, scarcity, and high toxicity of cobalt resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Among the rechargeable battery systems beyond LIBs, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) stand out as promising alternatives to LIBs because of low cost, abundance, and high sustainability of sodium resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 3 to 6">(3−6)</a> Moreover, Na shares similar chemical and physical properties with Li, so a rich variety of electrode materials, which exhibit excellent performance in LIBs, extend their performance to NIBs, including sulfur, selenium, metal sulfides, graphite, tin, phosphorus, hard carbons, and so forth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 7 to 22">(7−22)</a> In addition, various NIB cathodes such as sodium transition-metal oxides and sodium transition-metal phosphates are designed and synthesized based on their lithium transition-metal oxide and lithium transition-metal phosphate counterparts. However, with the larger radius (Na<sup>+</sup> 1.02 Å vs Li<sup>+</sup> 0.76 Å) and more complicated electrochemistry in NIBs, the observed electrochemical performance of these inorganic electrode materials in NIBs is not comparable to that in LIBs due to the large volume change and poor structure integrity upon long-term cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> This is especially evident, with regard to the specific capacity and cycle life. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> The development and applications of cost-effective and sustainable NIBs as next-generation energy storage devices are highly dependent on the performance of advanced electrode materials. Therefore, developing low-cost, high-capacity, long-lifetime, and high-efficiency electrode materials is paramount to the future of NIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">To date, great improvement is achieved for high-performance electrode materials in NIBs. A large number of high-capacity, stable, and high-efficiency cathode materials have been obtained, including P2–Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>1.1</sub>V<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7.9</sub>, P2–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>), and so forth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 26 to 31">(26−31)</a> In addition to the inorganic counterparts, crystalline and nanostructured organic materials, such as disodium rhodizonate, were also reported as high-capacity and stable cathode materials in NIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Among the anodes, hard carbon is the most promising because of its high specific capacity and stable cycle life. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> However, the hard carbon is produced by annealing the carbonaceous precursors at &gt;1000 °C under the inert gas, which is a time- and energy-consuming process. Moreover, to achieve high specific capacity, hard carbon anodes are discharged to ∼0 V because the discharge plateau of hard carbon anodes is close to 0 V. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> This will trigger the deposition of sodium metal on the surface of hard carbon anodes during the discharge, especially under high current density. Hence, it leads to the formation of sodium dendrites, which could cause the battery thermal runaway. To address this challenge, it is critical to search for advanced anode materials that deliver high specific capacities, stable cycle life, and a pair of redox plateaus in the cutoff window of 0.1–1 V.</div><div class="NLM_p">Among various anode materials, the diverse and tunable organic anode materials (OAMs) offer opportunities. As universal electrode materials for alkali-ion batteries, OAMs extend their high performance in LIBs to NIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> The rational structure design of OAMs is facilitated by their lightweight, low cost, abundance, high sustainability, and high structural tunability, resulting in cost-effective and sustainable energy storage materials for NIBs. Until now, the redox reactions of OAMs in organic NIBs follow five kinds of mechanisms, including carbonyl (C═O), imine (C═N), azo (N═N), thioketone/thioester (C═S), and anion insertion reactions. To obtain low-reaction-potential OAMs, carboxylate compounds based on carbonyl reactions could provide redox potential below 1.0 V. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 37 to 41">(37−41)</a> Due to the abundant and diverse nature of carboxylate compounds, their molecular structures can be manipulated to optimize their electrochemical performance as anodes in NIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">In this work, three halogenated carboxylate-based organic materials, disodium 2,5-difluoroterephthalate (DFTP-Na), disodium 2,5-dichloroterephthalate (DCTP-Na), and disodium 2,5-dibromoterephthalate (DBTP-Na) were prepared and used as OAMs for NIBs. A reported material, disodium terephthalate (DTP-Na), was used as a control to compare the electrochemical performance with the three synthesized compounds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The electron-rich halogen atoms are introduced into the carboxylate compounds to enhance the conductivity and interaction with Na ions. The structures of the four OAMs are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a–d. The mechanism of sodiation/desodiation process of these compounds is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> In NIBs, DFTP-Na shows the best electrochemical performance. It provides a reversible capacity of 212 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle, with the charge and discharge plateaus centered at 0.47 V. After 300 cycles, the reversible capacity of DFTP-Na anode is retained at 161 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a very low-capacity decay rate of 0.0802% per cycle. The outstanding electrochemical performance demonstrates that DFTP-Na is a promising organic anode material for NIBs.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/medium/am2c07383_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Molecular structures of (a) DTP-Na, (b) DFTP-Na, (c) DCTP-Na, and (d) DBTP-Na. (e) Redox reaction mechanism of these compounds.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07383&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The three as-synthesized halogenated OAMs, DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na, were characterized by multiple characterization techniques, including Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR), X-ray diffraction (XRD), Raman spectroscopy, and scanning electron microscopy (SEM). Based on the FTIR spectrum of DFTP-Na (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a), the sharp peak at the wavenumber of 1585 cm<sup>–1</sup> indicates the asymmetric stretching vibration of carboxylate groups in the DFTP-Na compound, while the sharp peak at 1407 cm<sup>–1</sup> represents the symmetric stretching vibration. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> These peaks also appear in the FTIR spectra of the other three compounds (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1a, S2a, and S3a</a>). This observation illustrates the existence of the carboxylate group in all the four organic materials. Moreover, in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a, the peak at 1177 cm<sup>–1</sup> demonstrates the presence of the aromatic C–F bond; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> similar peaks at 1071 and 1045 cm<sup>–1</sup> in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1a and S2a</a> prove the presence of C–Cl and C–Br bonds, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> In order to verify the results, Raman tests were utilized to measure the four anode materials. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, the peaks at 1628 and 1406 cm<sup>–1</sup> represent the asymmetric and symmetric stretching of the carboxylate group, and the peak at 672 cm<sup>–1</sup> indicates the appearance of the C–F bond. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> For DCTP-Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>), DBTP-Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>), and DTP-Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a>), the stretching vibration peaks for the carboxylate group and peaks for C–Cl and C–Br bonds can also be observed, which further prove the existence of carboxylic groups in the four organic materials and the corresponding carbon–halogen bonds in DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na. XRD technique was employed to study the crystallographic features of materials; in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1c, S2c, and S3c</a>, all these organic carboxylate compounds display crystalline structures. To further study the molecular structure of these three carboxylate compounds, <sup>1</sup>H NMR and <sup>13</sup>C NMR were applied for characterization with deuterium oxide (D<sub>2</sub>O) as the solvent. Based on the <sup>1</sup>H NMR of DFTP-Na (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d), the peak at 7.35 ppm indicates the two hydrogen atoms attached to the benzene ring of DFTP-Na; they have the same chemical shift as they are under the same chemical environment. In the <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e), the peak at 171.42 ppm represents the sp <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> carbons in the carboxylic groups, and the peaks at 117.09, 128.28, 153.92, and 156.34 ppm indicate the aromatic carbons in the benzene ring. Among them, both the peaks at 153.92 and 156.34 ppm represent the carbon attached with the fluorine, which is the C–F peak splitting due to the C–F coupling. This phenomenon can also be observed in the other <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectra of compounds that contain fluorine. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Similarly, the <sup>1</sup>H and <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectra of DCTP-Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1d,e</a>) and DBTP-Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2d,e</a>) also demonstrate their molecular structures; both compounds have one proton peak in <sup>1</sup>H NMR and four carbon peaks in <sup>13</sup>C NMR. To confirm the structure of DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na, mass spectrometry (MS) tests were carried out to measure their molecular weight. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, the MS results of DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na are 247.1, 279.1, and 368.7, which well matched with their molecular weight. The results demonstrate that the three expected compounds were successfully synthesized by the reaction. The morphology of the three synthesized materials is observed through SEM. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1f, S2f</a>, the DFTP-Na particle size is in the submicrometer-to-micrometer range, while DCTP-Na and DBTP-Na show a larger particle size from a few micrometers to tens of micrometers. The smaller particle size of DFTP-Na can facilitate its reaction kinetics and improve the electrochemical performance. With the results of characterization, it can be confirmed that all the four compounds contain carboxylate groups, and the three synthesized materials, DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na, contain the corresponding halogen atoms, and they are all composed of microsized particles.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/medium/am2c07383_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Characterization results of DFTP-Na. (a) FTIR spectrum, (b) Raman spectrum, (c) XRD pattern, (d) <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum, (e) <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectrum, and (f) SEM image.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07383&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To examine the electrochemical performance of the three synthesized materials and DTP-Na, these compounds were used as active materials in the anodes and coupled with the sodium metal as the counter electrode in NIBs. To identify a proper electrolyte system for the DFTP-Na anode, three different electrolytes, including 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME, 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME), and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC, were coupled with the DFTP-Na anodes and tested at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, the anode with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in the DEGDME electrolyte showed the highest capacity and best cyclic stability among the three electrolytes, indicating that 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEGDME is a proper electrolyte for the electrochemical tests of the DFTP-Na anode. Hence, 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEGDME was used as an electrolyte for all the electrochemical measurements. As shown in the galvanostatic charge–discharge curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a), DFTP-Na delivered a charge capacity of 212 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle at the current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a). However, the Coulombic efficiency (CE) of the first cycle is only 40%, which is due to the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) in the first cycle. The irreversible capacity causes the large capacity loss of the electrode. In addition, the curve shows a pair of redox plateaus centered at 0.47 V. To exclude the capacity contribution from carbon black (CB), the reversible capacity of CB was measured at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> because the ratio of CB to DFTP-Na is 1:2 in the electrode, and the current density for the DFTP-Na anode is 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The CB electrode showed a reversible capacity of 38.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). The capacity contribution of CB was subtracted from the capacity of DFTP-Na and is presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. From the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curve (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b), it is observed that the charge plateau of DFTP-Na is at 0.61 V, while the discharge plateau is at 0.41 V. For the other three compounds, DCTP-Na, DBTP-Na, and DTP-Na displayed initial charge capacity and first-cycle CE of 154 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 39% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a</a>), 104 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 38% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8b</a>), and 139 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 58% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8c</a>) at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. It is obvious that all these compounds have low first-cycle CE, and DFTP-Na has the highest first-cycle charge capacity. In the long-term cyclic stability results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8d–f</a>), DFTP-Na retains a reversible capacity of 161 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c), corresponding to a slow capacity decay rate of 0.0802% per cycle. In addition, the CE of DFTP-Na is close to 100% upon long-term cycling. In contrast, DTP-Na, which was chosen for comparison, retains 58 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8f</a>) with a capacity decay rate of 0.29% per cycle. DCTP-Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8d</a>) and DBTP-Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8e</a>) also show poor capacity retention upon long-term cycling. Based on the results, DFTP-Na showed both the highest reversible capacity and the highest cyclic stability. Therefore, it was chosen for further electrochemical tests. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d shows the charge–discharge curves of DFTP-Na at various current densities from 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e shows the rate capability of DFTP-Na at different current densities. The current density is increased from 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the capacity is reduced from 236 to 81 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. When the current density is decreased back to 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible capacity recovers to 204 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with the capability recovery of 86%, demonstrating the robust rate capability of DFTP-Na. DFTP-Na outperforms DCTP-Na and DBTP-Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>) in terms of rate capability. It showed higher capability retention with increased current density up to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Moreover, after the current density is reduced back to 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, its capacity recovery rate of 86% is also the highest compared with DCTP-Na (75%) and DBTP-Na (70%). This result further proves that DFTP-Na is the most promising anode material among the three halogenated compounds. Based on the exceptional rate performance, a high current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> was applied for a high-rate and long cycling test. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e, DFTP-Na can retain a reversible capacity of 103.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles, which indicates excellent cycling stability in NIBs. The CE of DFTP-Na upon long-term cycling at current densities of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> is provided in the scale of 80–105% in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/medium/am2c07383_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performances of DFTP-Na. (a) Galvanostatic charge–discharge curves at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, (b) CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, (c) cycle life and CE at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, (d) galvanostatic charge–discharge curves at different current densities from 20 to 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, (e) rate capability test curve at various current densities, and (f) high current density test at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07383&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further study the reaction kinetics of DFTP-Na, CV tests at various scan rates, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) were employed. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a, the cell with DFTP-Na was scanned from 0.1 to 2.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The peak currents at different scan rates were obtained, and the relationship between the natural logarithm of scan rates and the corresponding peak currents was plotted. The linear fits of relationships of anodic and cathodic peaks are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b. The <i>b</i> values of cathodic and anodic peaks are 0.8699 and 0.8371, respectively, which are closer to 1. This means the partial capacitive behavior dominates the redox reaction of the DFTP-Na anode, contributing to the high rate capability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c, the EIS test results show that the interfacial resistance of the pristine DFTP-Na anode is ∼30 Ω. It is reduced to 16 Ω after the first cycle and gradually increased to 18 Ω after 10 cycles. The interfacial resistance was stabilized at 18 Ω after 50 cycles. The small and stable interfacial resistance upon cycling indicates the stable SEI layer in the DFTP-Na anode, which is critical for the high cyclic stability. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d displays the results of the GITT test. The charge and discharge potentials of DFTP-Na are centered at 0.6 and 0.4 V, respectively. In the regions of charge and discharge plateaus, the overpotentials are 33 and 46 mV, respectively. These small overpotentials further indicate the fast reaction kinetics of the DFTP-Na anode.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/medium/am2c07383_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Examinations of reaction kinetics of DFTP-Na. (a) CV curves at scan rates from 0.1 to 2.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, (b) relationship between the natural logarithm of scan rates and the corresponding peak currents, (c) EIS tests of the pristine electrode and electrodes after different cycles, and (d) GITT test at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07383&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To exploit the interfacial structure of the halogenated carboxylate organic anodes, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was utilized to measure the pristine and cycled anodes. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a</a>, the peak at 284.7 eV for the graphitic carbon in the C 1s spectrum of the pristine DFTP-Na anode is used as a reference peak. The peaks at 286.2 and 288.4 eV stand for the C–O and C═O groups, respectively. The peak at 290 eV represents the C–F group. The C 1s spectrum of DFTP-Na after 10 cycles is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11b</a>. Compared with the peak for graphitic carbon, the intensity ratio of C–O and C═O peaks become higher, which indicates the appearance of C–O and C═O groups in SEI of the cycled DFTP-Na anode. Similarly, in the C 1s spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>) of the pristine and cycled DCTP-Na and DBTP-Na anodes, the same change of C–O and C═O peaks is observed. In the XPS spectrum of F 1s of the pristine DFTP-Na anode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12a</a>), the peak at 688 eV indicates the fluorine in the C–F group. After 10 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12b</a>), a new F 1s peak at 685.2 eV appears, representing the fluorine of NaF in the SEI layer. In the F 1s spectra of the cycled DCTP-Na and DBTP-Na anodes, there is also a sharp NaF peak (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figurse S12d,f</a>). However, the ratio of fluorine compounds in the three SEI layers is different. The peak ratio of NaF to C–F in the cycled DFTP-Na anode is much higher than that in the cycled DCTP-Na and DBTP-Na anodes, indicating a higher NaF content in the SEI layer. As NaF is a very stable component in the SEI layer, a higher NaF content enhances the stability of the SEI layer, which contributes to the long cycle life of the DFTP-Na anode.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the molecular, crystalline, and morphological structure change after cycling, FTIR, XRD, SEM, and TEM were utilized to detect the DFTP-Na anodes. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, the peaks in the spectra did not change from the pristine DFTP-Na anode to the anode after 20 cycles, which indicates that the molecular structure of DFTP-Na is stable during the cycling process. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b shows the XRD tests from the pristine to 20th cycle anodes. The peaks in the spectra remain the same from the pristine to the 20th cycle, which shows the stable crystalline structure of DFTP-Na during the cycling. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c–f shows the SEM images of DFTP-Na anodes from the pristine to 10th cycle. It can be observed that the shape and size of DFTP-Na particles did not change during the cycling, which further proves the high stability of DFTP-Na in NIBs. To further investigate the cycling stability of the three anode materials, TEM tests were performed to analyze the morphology change of the three materials before cycling and after 10 cycles. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>, the change of the particle shape and size of the three materials before and after 10 cycles is minor, indicating good cycling stability. These results further prove that DFTP-Na is a promising organic anode material for stable and sustainable NIBs.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/medium/am2c07383_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Characterization tests of DFTP-Na electrodes before and after various cycles. (a) FTIR spectra, (b) XRD patterns; SEM images of (c) pristine, (d) 1st, (e) 5th, and (f) 10th electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07383&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To better understand the enhanced performance of DFTP-Na, we performed DFT computational simulations (see Computational Methods). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> provides the energy gap between the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) and lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO), energy levels of the HOMO and LUMO, and redox potentials of all molecules in this study. Previous studies have proposed that the energy gap between the HOMO and LUMO (ε<sub>gap</sub>) for a molecule is a good indicator of battery performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> Functionalizing DTP-Na with halogen atoms lowers the energy of the LUMO but increases the energy of the HOMO relative to DTP-Na (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>). DBTP-Na has the smallest overall HOMO–LUMO gap of the functionalized molecules, with DFTP-Na (4.63 eV) being the largest. DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na have smaller gaps than DTP-Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). Interestingly, previous research has proposed that the addition of strong electron-withdrawing groups should aid to use DTP-Na as an anode. It has also been reported that a smaller energy gap (ε<sub>gap</sub>) between HOMO and LUMO for a molecule is a good indicator of battery performance. This would imply that DBTP-Na should exhibit a better overall performance compared to DFTP-Na, which is not the case. Our results suggest that it is more than electronic levels that play a role in the overall performance and that the formation of a stable NaF-rich SEI layer and the halogen in the organic electrode materials may play larger roles in the sodiation process.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/medium/am2c07383_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Energies of the HOMO and LUMO for DTP-Na (left), DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na. The C, H, O, Na, Cl, Br and F atoms are gray, white, red, silver, pink, brown, and green, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07383&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the interactions throughout the cycle life of the molecule, we added one sodium atom to DTP-Na and DFTP-Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>). We used the interaction of a third sodium atom to better understand the improved performance of DTP-Na compared to DFTP-Na. The third sodium atom interacts with DTP-Na by forming a Na–O bond with the carbonyl group (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>). Surprisingly, the addition of a third sodium atom to DFTP-Na interacts quite differently. While the third sodium atom does interact with one oxygen on the carbonyl group, it also interacts weakly with one fluorine atom substituted on the ring. The addition of electronegative fluorine atoms to DTP-Na provides an additional means of Na interacting with the molecule throughout the mechanistic process and thereby enhances the overall performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">The average redox plateaus of DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na are 0.47, 0.51, and 0.6 V, respectively, according to their charge–discharge curves. The reduction potentials of organic electrode materials are related to the LUMO energy level. A lower LUMO energy level indicates a greater electron affinity and a higher reduction potential. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> As shown in the results of the DFT calculation, DBTP-Na has the lowest LUMO of −1.509 eV among the three halogenated carboxylates, which means it has the best electron affinity and therefore the highest reduction potential. In contrast, DFTP-Na has the highest LUMO of −1.457 eV and thus the lowest reduction potential among the three halogenated carboxylates. The results demonstrate that introducing F into sodium carboxylates is better than that of Cl and Br in terms of retaining the low reduction potential of OAMs.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further study the reaction mechanism, <i>ex situ</i> Raman spectroscopy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a) and XRD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b) of DFTP-Na at different charge/discharge potential stages were performed. As the cycled electrodes were air-sensitive, they were sealed in glass vials in a glovebox before the Raman tests. For XRD tests, a polyimide (PI) tape was used to isolate the electrodes from the air, and the tape has a large broad peak from 10 to 30° (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a, the peaks at ∼1400 and ∼1600 cm<sup>–1</sup> disappeared after the electrode was discharged to 0.1 V, indicating the disappearance of carboxylate groups after the sodiation of DFTP-Na. After charging the anode back to 0.5 V, these two peaks reappeared but were weak, due to the desodiation of the anode. After charging back to 2 V, the two peaks were recovered, indicating the complete desodiation of DFTP-Na. This result proves the redox reaction mechanism of carboxylate groups in DFTP-Na upon cycling, as discussed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e. In the sodium carboxylates (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a–d), there are two Na ions in the carboxylate groups, which are not electrochemically active and cannot be extracted during the charge process. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e, the reaction mechanism follows a two-electron/Na-ion redox reaction. Two external Na ions and electrons will react with sodium carboxylates to generate sodiated carboxylates during the discharge process. Then, these two Na ions and electrons can be extracted from the sodiated carboxylates during the charge process. The original two Na ions in the carboxylates will not participate in the redox reaction to retain the structural stability of carboxylate-based anode materials during the discharge/charge process. <i>Ex situ</i> XRD was further used to monitor the crystalline structure evolution of the first cycle. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b, the DFTP-Na electrode at 0.1 V shows a large difference from the pristine electrode, indicating a crystal structure change after sodiation. Most XRD peaks can be recovered after charging back to 2 V, while the peak at ∼30° disappears, probably because it is weakened and overlapped with the peak from the PI tap. Therefore, the <i>ex situ</i> Raman and XRD results further confirm the redox reaction between the carboxylate groups in DFTP-Na and sodium ions, as well as the high reversibility of the reaction.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/medium/am2c07383_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. <i>Ex situ</i> tests of DFTP-Na electrodes at different potential stages. (a) <i>Ex situ</i> Raman spectra and (b) <i>ex situ</i> XRD patterns.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/large/am2c07383_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c07383&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the carboxylate compound, DFTP-Na, is a promising organic anode material for stable and sustainable NIBs. Compared with the other two compounds (DCTP-Na and DBTP-Na) and the control sample (DTP-Na), DFTP-Na provides a much higher initial reversible capacity of 212 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a pair of redox plateaus centered at 0.47 V and can retain a reversible capacity of 161 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles with a slow capacity decay rate of 0.0802% per cycle, which are the best among the four carboxylate compounds. In addition to the high capacity and long cycle life, DFTP-Na also exhibits high rate capability up to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Computational results indicate that the enhanced performance of DFTP-Na is due to the fluorination and HOMO–LUMO gap when compared to DTP-Na and DBTP-Na/DCTP-Na, respectively. The reaction kinetics and molecular and crystalline structures of DFTP-Na were examined by CV, EIS, GITT, FTIR, XRD, and SEM, which indicate that DFTP-Na has a stable molecular and crystalline structure upon cycling and forms a stable SEI layer. These features lead to the high stability and excellent electrochemical performance of DFTP-Na as an anode material in NIBs. Besides the substituent effect, the electrochemical performance of organic electrode materials is also influenced by other factors, such as the particle size, the packing state of organic molecules, weak intermolecular interactions, and so forth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 54 and 55">(54,55)</a> As promising future research directions, more work will be conducted to study the impacts of these factors on the performance of organic batteries, which will provide guidance for the rational design of high-performance organic electrode materials in sustainable batteries.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">2, 5-Dichloroterephthalic acid and 2, 5-dibromoterephthalic acid were purchased from TCI America; 2, 5-difluoroterephthalic acid was bought from Alfa Chemistry; disodium terephthalate (DTP-Na) was bought from Alfa Aesar; and they were all used as received. The preparation of DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na was as follows: 2, 5-difluoroterephthalic acid, 2, 5-dichloroterephthalic acid, or 2, 5-dibromoterephthalic acid was dispersed in absolute ethanol with sodium hydroxide in 5% excess. The solutions were stirred for 24 h under room temperature; then, the precipitates were collected by filtration. The three precipitates (DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na) were then washed with absolute ethanol and dried in a vacuum oven overnight at 100 °C. The DFTP-Na product was gray powder, while DCTP-Na and DBTP-Na were white powders.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Material Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">XRD pattern was recorded by a Rigaku MiniFlex 600 system using CuKα radiation; Raman measurements were performed on a Horiba Jobin Yvon Labram Aramis using a 532 nm diode-pumped solid-state laser, attenuated to give ∼900 μW power at the sample surface; FTIR was recorded by an Agilent Cary 630 FTIR instrument; SEM images were taken by a JEOL JSM-IT500HR InTouchScope SEM instrument (Japan); NMR tests were performed with a Bruker Ascend 400 NMR spectrometer. MS tests were done with Sciex QTRAP 4500. Samples were prepared at a concentration of 1 μg/mL in water and directly infused into the mass spectrometer. TEM images were taken by JEOL JEM-1400F; XPS measurements were carried out on a PHI 5000 VersaProbe II (Physical Electronics) spectrometer, which is equipped with a hemispherical analyzer. The spectrometer is attached to the Ar glovebox, and sample transfer was directly through this to avoid any contact of the samples with air and moisture. Monochromatic Al-Kα excitation (hν = 1486.6 eV) was used at a power of 25 W, additionally applying a low-energy electron charge neutralizer. The high-resolution spectrum of each element was collected with a pass energy of 23.25 eV in an analysis area of 100*100 μm.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p">The product compounds were blended with CB and a sodium alginate (SA) binder to form a slurry with the weight ratio of 6:3:1. The electrodes were prepared by casting the slurry onto a copper foil using a doctor blade and dried in a vacuum oven at 100 °C overnight. The copper foil coated with the slurry was punched into rounded electrodes with an area mass loading of ∼1.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The sodium batteries used for electrochemical tests were coin cells and assembled with sodium metal as the counter electrode, 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DEGDME electrolyte and glass-fiber as the separator. The electrochemical performance of the cells was tested by an Arbin battery test station (LBT20084, Arbin Instruments, USA). Cyclic voltammograms were examined with Gamry Interface 1010 E Potentiostat/Galvanostat/ZRA, with scan rates from 0.1 to 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Impedance analysis was performed by the same Gamry instrument. In the GITT test, the battery was discharged to 0.1 V and then charged back to 2 V at the current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. During the cycling process, the battery was discharged or charged for 30 min and then rested for 3 h to reach the equilibrium potentials. High mass loading electrodes were applied for XRD tests in order to enhance the strength of the signal of XRD, which were fabricated by using polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) as the binder, and the ratio of DFTP-Na, CB, and PTFE was still 6:3:1. To avoid the influence of carboxyl group in SA, the binder of electrodes utilized in <i>ex situ</i> Raman tests was changed to polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF), and the ratio of DFTP-Na, CB, and PVDF was changed to 8:1:1 to decrease the effect of CB in Raman tests. The ratio of electrodes in <i>ex situ</i> XRD tests was also changed to 8:1:1.</div><div class="NLM_p">The theoretical capacity values of DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na were calculated based on the equation<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/medium/am2c07383_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span>where <i>n</i> is the number of electrons transferred, <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant, and <i>M</i> is the molar mass of the molecule. Based on the reaction mechanism, all the three halogenated sodium carboxylates undergo a two-electron reaction; thus, <i>n</i> is 2. For DFTP-Na, its molar mass is 246.11 g mol<sup>–1</sup>; thus, its theoretical capacity is 217.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Similarly, the theoretical capacity values of DCTP-Na (279.02 g mol<sup>–1</sup>) and DBTP-Na (367.92 g mol<sup>–1</sup>) are 192.1 and 145.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Based on the GITT results, the diffusion coefficient can be found according to the simplified equation of Fick’s second law <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/asset/images/medium/am2c07383_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span>where τ is the pulse time, <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> is the molar volume of the active material, <i>m</i><sub>b</sub> is the mass of the electrode material, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> is the molar mass of the active material, <i>S</i> is the contact area, and <i>L</i> is the average thickness of the electrode. The mass of the electrode material used is 1.5 mg, and the molar mass of DFTP-Na is 246.11 g mol<sup>–1</sup>. The diameter of the electrode is 7/16 inch, which is 1.11 cm. Thus, the contact area is 0.968 cm<sup>2</sup>. The thickness of the electrode was measured by SEM (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>) to be 12.1 μm. The shape of the active material can be regarded as a cylinder, so the volume of the material is 1.17 × 10<sup>–3</sup> cm<sup>3</sup>. As DFTP-Na forms 60% mass of the material, which is 0.9 mg, the molar volume <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> is 319.9 mL mol<sup>–1</sup>. From the data of GITT, it is observed that τ is 3 h, Δ<i>E</i><sub><i>S</i></sub> is 0.03 V, and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> is 0.02 V. Therefore, based on all the measured and calculated data, the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> can be found as 1.08 × 10<sup>–9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Computational Methods</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Density functional theory calculations were carried out using the Amsterdam Density Functional (ADF) 2020 package at the B3LYP/TZ2P level of theory. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 52 and 53">(52,53)</a> We began with terephthalic acid as our starting structure and replaced the hydrogen atoms on the carboxyl group with sodium atoms. We substituted two hydrogens on the ring with the respective halide atom (<i>i.e.</i>, F, Cl, or Br) and performed a geometry optimization for each structure. To ascertain the impact of adding sodium atom to molecules, we added one sodium atom consecutively and performed geometry optimizations on each molecule. For geometry optimization, all atoms were allowed to move freely until the energy and gradient convergences were below the 0.00018 and 0.00067 Å thresholds, respectively. All geometry optimizations were performed with molecules in the lowest possible spin state (<i>i.e.</i>, singlet or doublet) with spin polarization. A frequency analysis was performed to ensure that the molecule was in the ground state (<i>i.e.</i>, ensuring no negative frequencies).</div></div><div id="sec4_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Statistical Analysis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Statistical analysis was performed to further study the particle size of DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na by using their SEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16a–d</a>). The discussion for the particle size distribution, mean, and standard deviation is provided in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. In addition, the statistical results of the particle size are also shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3–S5</a> of the Supporting Information.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c07383?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c07383</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">FTIR spectra, Raman spectra, XRD patterns, <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectra, <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectra, and SEM images of DCTP-Na and DBTP-Na; FTIR spectrum, Raman spectrum, and XRD pattern of DTP-Na; MS spectra of DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na; charge–discharge curves, cycle life, and CE of DFTP-Na in different electrolytes; charge–discharge curves, cycle life, and CE of CB; cycle life and CE of DFTP-Na without CB contribution; charge–discharge curves, cycle life, and CE of DCTP-Na, DBTP-Na, and DTP-Na; rate capability of DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na; CE of DFTP-Na upon long-term cycling; C 1s and F 1s XPS spectra of pristine and cycled DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na anodes; TEM images of pristine and cycled DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na; images of DTP-Na and DFTP-Na interacting with an additional sodium atom from DFT calculations; energy-level values for the HOMO, LUMO, and HOMO–LUMO gap; SEM image of a DFTP-Na electrode showing its thickness; comparison of DFTP-Na with other previous works; calculation process; SEM images of statistical analysis; and statistical analyses of DFTP-Na, DCTP-Na, and DBTP-Na particles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c07383/suppl_file/am2c07383_si_001.pdf">am2c07383_si_001.pdf (1.68 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c07383" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhBCAqlndWe_A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40847g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpqNKJTie6WmbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhBCAqlndWe_A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtFOisrzJ&amp;md5=2e02460553edc21e1ea8b73982ed63bb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Slater, Michael D.; Kim, Donghan; Lee, Eungje; Doeff, Marca; Johnson, Christopher S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">23</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">947-958</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgIRVsvY7asHA"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard carbons for sodium-ion batteries: Structure, analysis, sustainability, and electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dou, Xinwei; Hasa, Ivana; Saurel, Damien; Vaalma, Christoph; Wu, Liming; Buchholz, Daniel; Bresser, Dominic; Komaba, Shinichi; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Today (Oxford, United Kingdom)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">23</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">87-104</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">MTOUAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1369-7021</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Hard carbons are extensively studied for application as anode materials in sodium-ion batteries, but only recently a great interest has been focused toward the understanding of the sodium storage mechanism and the comprehension of the structure-function correlation.  Although several interesting mechanisms have been proposed, a general mechanism explaining the obsd. electrochem. processes is still missing, which is essentially originating from the remaining uncertainty on the complex hard carbons structure.  The achievement of an in-depth understanding of the processes occurring upon sodiation, however, is of great importance for a rational design of optimized anode materials.In this review, we aim at providing a comprehensive overview of the up-to-date known structural models of hard carbons and their correlation with the proposed models for the sodium-ion storage mechanisms.  In this regard, a particular focus is set on the most powerful anal. tools to study the structure of hard carbons (upon de-/sodiation) and a crit. discussion on how to interpret and perform such anal.  Targeting the eventual commercialization of hard carbon anodes for sodium-ion batteries - after having established a fundamental understanding - we close this review with a careful evaluation of potential strategies to ensure a high degree of sustainability, since this is undoubtedly a crucial parameter to take into account for the future large-scale prodn. of hard carbons.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoglanyMMwzlbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgIRVsvY7asHA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXksV2isg%253D%253D&amp;md5=f27d15bc7c58afaf33ea74d47395ffc2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1039/c8ee01023d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Progress on Sodium Ion Batteries: Potential High-Performance Anodes</span>. <i>Energy Environ.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Due to massively growing demand arising from energy storage systems, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been recognized as the most attractive alternative to the current commercialized lithium ion batteries (LIBs) owing to the wide availability and accessibility of sodium.  Unfortunately, the low energy d., inferior power d. and poor cycle life are still the main issues for SIBs in the current drive to push the entire technol. forward to meet the benchmark requirements for commercialization.  Over the past few years, tremendous efforts have been devoted to improving the performance of SIBs, in terms of higher energy d. and longer cycling lifespans, by optimizing the electrode structure or the electrolyte compn.  In particular, among the established anode systems, those materials, such as metals/alloys, phosphorus/phosphides, and metal oxides/sulfides/selenides, that typically deliver high theor. sodium-storage capacities have received growing interest and achieved significant progress.  Although some review articles on electrodes for SIBs have been published already, many new reports on these anode materials are constantly emerging, with more promising electrochem. performance achieved via novel structural design, surface modification, electrochem. performance testing techniques, etc.  So, we herein summarize the most recent developments on these high-performance anode materials for SIBs in this review.  Furthermore, the different reaction mechanisms, the challenges assocd. with these materials, and effective approaches to enhance performance are discussed.  The prospects for future high-energy anodes in SIBs are also discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrQBTNlAIykC7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgIRVsvY7asHA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhtlOjt7jK&amp;md5=e4580fd3c5fcf8eb8cfa08a8dc758f49</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201915917"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luan, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hybrid materials, integrating the merits of individual components, are ideal structures for efficient sodium storage.  However, the construction of hybrid structures with decent phys./electrochem. properties is still challenging.  Now, the elaborate design and synthesis of hierarchical nanoboxes composed of three-layered Cu2S@carbon@MoS2 as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries is reported.  Through a facile multistep template-engaged strategy, ultrathin MoS2 nanosheets are grown on nitrogen-doped carbon-coated Cu2S nanoboxes to realize the Cu2S@carbon@MoS2 configuration.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Organic Electrode Materials for Metal Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shea, John J.; Luo, Chao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Org. and polymer materials have been extensively investigated as electrode materials for rechargeable batteries because of the low cost, abundance, environmental benignity, and high sustainability.  To date, org. electrode materials have been applied in a large variety of energy storage devices, including nonaq. Li-ion, Na-ion, K-ion, dual-ion, multivalent-metal, aq., all-solid-state, and redox flow batteries, because of the universal properties of org. electrode materials.  Moreover, some org. materials enable the batteries to be operated in the extreme conditions, such as a wide temp. range (-70 to 150°), a wide pH range, and in the presence of O2.  As a guidance for the research in org. batteries, this review focuses on the reaction mechanisms and applications of org. electrode materials.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">MoS2 Nanoflowers with Expanded Interlayers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Zhe; Wang, Lixiu; Zhang, Kai; Wang, Jianbin; Cheng, Fangyi; Tao, Zhanliang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">MoS2 nanoflowers with expanded interlayer spacing of the (002) plane were synthesized and used as high-performance anode in Na-ion batteries.  By controlling the cut-off voltage to the range of 0.4-3 V, an intercalation mechanism rather than a conversion reaction is taking place.  The MoS2 nanoflower electrode shows high discharge capacities of 350 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1, 300 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1, and 195 mAh g-1 at 10 A g-1.
